<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Two services I cannot live without …]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2010/09/27/two-services-i-cannot-live-without/</link>
  <pubDate>Mon, 27 Sep 2010 00:23:12 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=5</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[There are two "cloud" services that I live by now:
<ul>
	<li><a href="http://www.dropbox.com/referrals/NTI1Mjc3MTI5">Dropbox</a> - simple, replicated, multi-platform file hosting.  Does one thing, and does it *really* well.</li>
	<li><a href="http://www.rememberthemilk.com">Remember the Milk</a> - online task management, with ability to share task lists.</li>
</ul>
Accessing these services from my Linux, Apple, and Windows PCs, *plus* my Android phone, really proves the power of the cloud, but even more proves the power of the small, innovative company.  Simple service, simple business model, incredible functionality.

I just wish these services were available for on-site installation, so I could keep my head out of the clouds...]]></content:encoded>

  <title><![CDATA[Windows Live Spaces moving to Wordpress.com !!!]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2010/09/28/windows-live-spaces-moving-to-wordpress-com/</link>
  <pubDate>Tue, 28 Sep 2010 13:01:13 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=8</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Wow - never would have <a href="http://en.blog.wordpress.com/2010/09/27/welcome-windows-live-spaces-bloggers/">imagined this</a>:
<blockquote>We’re excited to announce that WordPress.com is now the default blogging platform for Windows Live Spaces users. We’ve worked with our partners at Microsoft to create a simple migration service for Spaces bloggers to easily bring all their posts, comments, and photos to WordPress.com.</blockquote>]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Enterprise, Large Scale File Services]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2010/10/26/enterprise-large-scale-file-services/</link>
  <pubDate>Wed, 27 Oct 2010 01:28:57 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=45</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Admittedly, the world of file services has changed since Novell ruled the roost with Netware.  All sorts of new buzzwords exist: Web Content Management, Enterprise Document Management, Document Archiving, Knowledge Management ... but you still can't beat simple file storage service like Windows offers natively.  Except, it is really hard to provide that kind of service at a very large scale.
So here is the question -- how do I provide a file service with the following requirements:
<ul>
	<li>Must scale beyond 25,000 users (potentially 100,000), each with private "home" directories, plus whatever permutations of group space can be imagined.</li>
	<li>Must support large amounts of storage, including individual files of several hundred gigabytes, user/group quotas of several terabytes.</li>
	<li>Must support access from OSX, Windows, and Linux such that applications on these systems can natively open, read, write files -- in other words, similar to simple CIFS access, though a non-native client to support this functionality is acceptable.</li>
	<li>Must support some level of access from mobile devices, including Android, iPhone/iPad, Windows Mobile, and ideally Blackberry too.</li>
	<li>Must provide a rich "sexy-looking" web interface.</li>
	<li>Must provide consistent abstract interface -- in other words, scaling across hundreds of servers is acceptable, as long as users never need to be told "connect to server #17 for X, and server #53 for Y".  There should be some sort of abstracted virtual filesystem.</li>
	<li>Must support user-controllable ACLs to facilitate sharing and security.</li>
	<li>Must be accessible by non-technical end users with very little handholding - should be "intuitive".</li>
	<li>Must allow integration with backup solution that can provide file-level restoration.</li>
	<li>Should allow for storage of data to be accessed by Linux and Windows servers, such as user generated web content, HPC-generated research data, etc.</li>
	<li>Should allow for attachment of metadata for searching.</li>
	<li>Should allow integration with backup solution that allows end-user to perform file-level restoration.</li>
</ul>
Some have tried to convince me that Windows DFS can do all this, but I have yet to see a deployment that actually encompasses all of the above.  Anyone have any references?

I am quite intrigued by <a href="http://openafs.org/">OpenAFS</a>, using the <a href="http://www.filedrawers.org/">filedrawers</a> web interface, and possibly using the Samba gateway to avoid deploying the OpenAFS client to every machine -- anyone with any experience doing this?  Anyone serve OpenAFS data out over DAV via Apache, mod_dav, and mod_waklog?  Is filedrawers or DAV an acceptable mobile device access mechanism?  Pitfalls?

What else should I be considering?]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Linux Management and Monitoring: Zabbix + Puppet ?!?]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2010/12/31/linux-management-and-monitoring-zabbix-puppet/</link>
  <pubDate>Fri, 31 Dec 2010 20:06:40 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=62</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[I've spent a good part of this week evaluating various solutions for monitoring and managing Linux servers.  There are many solutions that meet some of the needs, but I have not found a single product that does everything I need.  Without going into significant detail, here are my needs:
<ul>
	<li>Simple service up/down monitoring and alerting</li>
	<li>Detailed metrics for disk, CPU, memory, network, etc</li>
	<li>Storage and graphing of historical data</li>
	<li>Flexible creation of custom monitors, triggers, etc.</li>
	<li>Significant pre-configured monitors, triggers, etc.</li>
	<li>Deployment/upgrade/removal of packages</li>
	<li>Configuration management</li>
	<li>Inventory of hardware/software</li>
	<li>Rich access control to satisfy various distributed administration and audit requirements</li>
	<li>*ALL* functionality exposed by web interface</li>
	<li>Inexpensive, preferably open source</li>
	<li>Able to scale to several hundred, maybe thousands of Linux servers - and possibly even Windows servers.</li>
	<li>Should be developed in a language I know so I can modify myself - (C, Perl, Python, Java)</li>
	<li>Should run on Debian, though it does not need to be in the repo.</li>
</ul>
<strong>Candidate solutions:</strong>
<ul>
	<li>Nagios</li>
	<li>OCS-Inventory</li>
	<li>Hyperic</li>
	<li>Puppet</li>
	<li>Zabbix</li>
</ul>
<strong>Conclusion:</strong>

Nothing definitive yet, but I'm narrowing in on a combination of Zabbix and Puppet.  I am still looking for something to automate the collection of inventory data, but I think this could be done using the Zabbix API to populate the Zabbix inventory (which is otherwise a manual process).

LazyWeb - any opinions?]]></content:encoded>

    <wp:comment_content><![CDATA[Hi,

We're using zabbix + puppet fro tribily.com and are overall very happy with it. 

One gotcha that will save you a lot fo time is that it is currently impossible (in any proper way) to install zabbix server from a puppet recipe on debian lenny using backports. 
It's a combination of the zabbix server package doing some nasties with dbconfig-common and the way preseeding works which is actually broken.

Other then that, highly recommended. For Puppet, there's foreman as a web interface, which is supposedly more advanced then the puppet dashboard. We haven't tried either of them so far, so I can give you no opinion. 

If you need more info, #zabbix on freenode and # puppet on the same are very good places to get info. You can also email me directly if you have specific questions. I can possibly give you some of our puppet recipes also. We are looking to opensource them soon, but currently they have too much private information in them :)

cheers,

Walter]]></wp:comment_content>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Convert Dropbox's Ubuntu package for Squeeze]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2011/04/17/convert-dropboxs-ubuntu-package-for-squeeze/</link>
  <pubDate>Sun, 17 Apr 2011 21:06:37 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=74</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[The Dropbox Ubuntu package <em>almost</em> works on Debian Squeeze ... except for one dependency with a numbering scheme that differs between Ubuntu and Debian.  Here is a quick script that extracts the package, corrects the one dependency in DEBIAN/control, and repackages, leaving a new .deb suitable for Squeeze.

convertDropbox4Squeeze:
<pre>#!/bin/sh
if [ $# -ne 1 ]; then
	echo "Usage: $0" 'nautilus-dropbox_$version_$arch.deb'
	exit 1
fi
DIR=`mktemp --directory`
mkdir -p $DIR/DEBIAN
dpkg-deb -x $1 $DIR/
dpkg-deb -e $1 $DIR/DEBIAN/
sed -i s/'libnautilus-extension1 (&gt;= 1:2.22.2)'/'libnautilus-extension1 (&gt;= 2.22.2)'/ ${DIR}/DEBIAN/control
dpkg-deb -b $DIR  ${1%.*}_squeeze.deb
rm -rf $DIR</pre>
Just download the .deb from the Dropbox site, then run
<pre>./convertDropbox4Squeeze nautilus_dropbox.......deb</pre>
And then just install the newly created nautilus_dropbx....squeeze.deb .]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[UCARP and Squeeze]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2011/09/10/ucarp-and-squeeze/</link>
  <pubDate>Sat, 10 Sep 2011 22:53:11 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=84</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[IP fault tolerance on Debian Squeeze couldn't be easier than with <a href="http://www.ucarp.org/project/ucarp">UCARP</a>!:
<ul>
	<li>Install UCARP on all nodes:
<pre>apt-get install ucarp</pre>
</li>
	<li>Edit /etc/network/interfaces on all nodes, replacing &lt;CLUSTER IP&gt; and &lt;PASSWORD&gt; as appropriate:
<pre>iface eth0 inet static
...
ucarp-vid 3
ucarp-vip &lt;CLUSTER IP&gt;
ucarp-password &lt;PASSWORD&gt;
ucarp-xparam -n
iface eth0:ucarp inet static
address &lt;CLUSTER IP&gt;
netmask 255.255.255.255</pre>
</li>
	<li>Reboot, and you're done!  &lt;CLUSTER IP&gt; will now be available from one of the nodes in your cluster at all times!</li>
</ul>
A few notes:
<ul>
	<li>All nodes in your UCARP cluster must be in the same subnet, and the shared cluster IP must also be in the same subnet.</li>
	<li>UCARP does not do cluster resource management for you, but it does provide hooks.  You will need to write some scripts to handle unmounting/mounting of shared storage, etc.</li>
	<li>UCARP does not detect service failures, only whole-host (or IP stack) failures.  But if you can detect the failure, simply send SIGUSR2 to the ucarp process to force into passive mode, forcing another node to become master.</li>
</ul>
<div>All-in-all, about as simple and sufficient as one can get!</div>]]></content:encoded>

  <title><![CDATA[A new Linux Admin is born!]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2011/10/06/a-new-linux-admin-is-born/</link>

  <content:encoded><![CDATA[[caption id="attachment_104" align="aligncenter" width="300" caption="Amber Grace Smith"]<a href="http://forsetti.files.wordpress.com/2011/10/2011-10-06-11-19-44.jpg"><img class="size-medium wp-image-104" title="Amber Grace Smith" src="http://forsetti.files.wordpress.com/2011/10/2011-10-06-11-19-44.jpg?w=300" alt="" width="300" height="225" /></a>[/caption]

Amber Grace Smith was born at 12:31 on October 4 2011, weighing 7 lbs 11 oz and measuring 20 in. Mother and baby are doing great and are returning home today.

Amber has spent her first few days reviewing the <a href="https://github.com/forsetti/mod_auth_cas">mod_auth_cas</a> source code, and hopes to work on the Linux Kernel crypto modules in the near future.

Welcome to the world, Amber!]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Convert Dropbox’s Ubuntu package for Squeeze]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2011/04/17/convert-dropboxs-ubuntu-package-for-squeeze-2/</link>
  <pubDate>Sun, 17 Apr 2011 21:06:37 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=74</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<p>The Dropbox Ubuntu package <em>almost</em> works on Debian Squeeze &#8230; except for one dependency with a numbering scheme that differs between Ubuntu and Debian.  Here is a quick script that extracts the package, corrects the one dependency in DEBIAN/control, and repackages, leaving a new .deb suitable for Squeeze.</p>
<p>convertDropbox4Squeeze:</p>
<pre>#!/bin/sh
if [ $# -ne 1 ]; then
	echo "Usage: $0" 'nautilus-dropbox_$version_$arch.deb'
	exit 1
fi
DIR=`mktemp --directory`
mkdir -p $DIR/DEBIAN
dpkg-deb -x $1 $DIR/
dpkg-deb -e $1 $DIR/DEBIAN/
sed -i s/'libnautilus-extension1 (&gt;= 1:2.22.2)'/'libnautilus-extension1 (&gt;= 2.22.2)'/ ${DIR}/DEBIAN/control
dpkg-deb -b $DIR  ${1%.*}_squeeze.deb
rm -rf $DIR</pre>
<p>Just download the .deb from the Dropbox site, then run</p>
<pre>./convertDropbox4Squeeze nautilus_dropbox.......deb</pre>
<p>And then just install the newly created nautilus_dropbx&#8230;.squeeze.deb .</p>
<br />  <a rel="nofollow" href="http://feeds.wordpress.com/1.0/gocomments/forsetti.wordpress.com/74/"><img alt="" border="0" src="http://feeds.wordpress.com/1.0/comments/forsetti.wordpress.com/74/" /></a> <img alt="" border="0" src="http://stats.wordpress.com/b.gif?host=forsetti.wordpress.com&#038;blog=16100127&amp;%23038;post=74&amp;%23038;subd=forsetti&amp;%23038;ref=&amp;%23038;feed=1" width="1" height="1" />]]></content:encoded>
  <excerpt:encoded><![CDATA[The Dropbox Ubuntu package almost works on Debian Squeeze &#8230; except for one dependency with a numbering scheme that differs between Ubuntu and Debian.  Here is a quick script that extracts the package, corrects the one dependency in DEBIAN/control, and repackages, leaving a new .deb suitable for Squeeze. convertDropbox4Squeeze: #!/bin/sh if [ $# -ne 1 [...]<img alt="" border="0" src="http://stats.wordpress.com/b.gif?host=forsetti.wordpress.com&amp;blog=16100127&amp;post=74&amp;subd=forsetti&amp;ref=&amp;feed=1" width="1" height="1" />]]></excerpt:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Linux Management and Monitoring: Zabbix + Puppet ?!?]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2010/12/31/linux-management-and-monitoring-zabbix-puppet-2/</link>
  <pubDate>Fri, 31 Dec 2010 20:06:40 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=62</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<p>I&#8217;ve spent a good part of this week evaluating various solutions for monitoring and managing Linux servers.  There are many solutions that meet some of the needs, but I have not found a single product that does everything I need.  Without going into significant detail, here are my needs:</p>
<ul>
<li>Simple service up/down monitoring and alerting</li>
<li>Detailed metrics for disk, CPU, memory, network, etc</li>
<li>Storage and graphing of historical data</li>
<li>Flexible creation of custom monitors, triggers, etc.</li>
<li>Significant pre-configured monitors, triggers, etc.</li>
<li>Deployment/upgrade/removal of packages</li>
<li>Configuration management</li>
<li>Inventory of hardware/software</li>
<li>Rich access control to satisfy various distributed administration and audit requirements</li>
<li>*ALL* functionality exposed by web interface</li>
<li>Inexpensive, preferably open source</li>
<li>Able to scale to several hundred, maybe thousands of Linux servers &#8211; and possibly even Windows servers.</li>
<li>Should be developed in a language I know so I can modify myself &#8211; (C, Perl, Python, Java)</li>
<li>Should run on Debian, though it does not need to be in the repo.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Candidate solutions:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Nagios</li>
<li>OCS-Inventory</li>
<li>Hyperic</li>
<li>Puppet</li>
<li>Zabbix</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Conclusion:</strong></p>
<p>Nothing definitive yet, but I&#8217;m narrowing in on a combination of Zabbix and Puppet.  I am still looking for something to automate the collection of inventory data, but I think this could be done using the Zabbix API to populate the Zabbix inventory (which is otherwise a manual process).</p>
<p>LazyWeb &#8211; any opinions?</p>
<br />  <a rel="nofollow" href="http://feeds.wordpress.com/1.0/gocomments/forsetti.wordpress.com/62/"><img alt="" border="0" src="http://feeds.wordpress.com/1.0/comments/forsetti.wordpress.com/62/" /></a> <img alt="" border="0" src="http://stats.wordpress.com/b.gif?host=forsetti.wordpress.com&#038;blog=16100127&amp;%23038;post=62&amp;%23038;subd=forsetti&amp;%23038;ref=&amp;%23038;feed=1" width="1" height="1" />]]></content:encoded>

  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Took me 15 minutes of searching and trying, but I eventually figured out how to get all posts tagged "linux" from my personal blog to be cross-posted here.

Check out the <a href="http://wordpress.org/extend/plugins/feedwordpress/">FeedWordPress</a> plugin for WordPress -- simply supply the remote RSS URL, and FeedWordPress will import posts directly into your WordPress site.

&nbsp;]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Using the UITS SSH Gateway]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2012/06/09/using-the-uits-ssh-gateway/</link>
  <pubDate>Sat, 09 Jun 2012 16:42:23 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://linux.uits.uconn.edu/mas02041/?p=90</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Adding the following to your ~/.ssh/config will cause all SSH access to servers named *.uits.uconn.edu to hop through ssh.uits.uconn.edu, authenticating as your NetID.  Note that if you have kinit'd as &lt;NETID&gt;/admin, or if you have copied your public SSH key to ssh.uits.uconn.edu and are using ssh-agent, this will be transparent.
<pre>Host ssh.uits.uconn.edu
    ProxyCommand none

Host *.uits.uconn.edu
    ProxyCommand ssh -A &lt;NETID&gt;@ssh.uits.uconn.edu exec nc %h %p</pre>]]></content:encoded>

  <title><![CDATA[Adopt the cloud, kill your IT career]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2012/06/12/adopt-the-cloud-kill-your-it-career/</link>
  <pubDate>Tue, 12 Jun 2012 01:00:20 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://linux.uits.uconn.edu/mas02041/?p=98</guid>

  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<a href="http://www.infoworld.com/print/195144">http://www.infoworld.com/print/195144</a>
<blockquote>...You might be eager to relinquish responsibility of a cranky infrastructure component and push the headaches to a cloud vendor, but in reality you aren't doing that at all. Instead, you're adding another avenue for the blame to follow. The end result of a catastrophic failure or data loss event is exactly the same whether you own the service or contract it out. The difference is you can't do anything about it directly. You jump out of the plane and hope that whoever packed your parachute knew what he or she was doing....</blockquote>]]></content:encoded>

  <title><![CDATA[Simplified VDI with Red Hat Enterprise Virtualization for Desktops]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2012/06/27/simplified-vdi-with-red-hat-enterprise-virtualization-for-desktops/</link>
  <pubDate>Wed, 27 Jun 2012 15:08:12 +0000</pubDate>

  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://linux.uits.uconn.edu/mas02041/?p=123</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[The Red Hat VDI solution has come a long way ... client-side rendering and media off-load, built right on top of the RHEV stack (no separate infrastructure!), user portal is part of the package (no additional purchase!).

Comparisons between VMWare View and XenDesktop show roughly functionality/feature parity, but Red Hat VDI appears *much* less expensive, and can provide both Windows and Linux virtual desktops.

<a href="http://www.redhat.com/summit/sessions/index.html#5">http://www.redhat.com/summit/sessions/index.html#5</a>

And checkout the Red Hat TCO/ROI comparison tool:

<a href="https://roianalyst.alinean.com/ent_02/AutoLogin.do?d=482903301639024770">https://roianalyst.alinean.com/ent_02/AutoLogin.do?d=482903301639024770</a>

However - a <strong>critical</strong> feature is still missing.  While Red Hat VDI looks like a <em>great</em> replacement for desktops, there is no iPad client yet.  For many, this may be the killer.  It is on the near-future roadmap though!]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[SELinux for Immortals]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2012/06/27/selinux-for-immortals/</link>
  <pubDate>Wed, 27 Jun 2012 17:44:44 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://linux.uits.uconn.edu/mas02041/?p=127</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[SELinux provides *very* strong sandboxing capabilities.  In very simplistic terms --- access control can now be applied not just at the filesystem, but also across network access, X access, enforced and automatic chroots that are cleaned-up when the process ends, all with fine-grained audit logging.

But -- this ain't your grandma's chmod.  There is some significant complexity.  But, depending in your risk model, it may very well be worth it.

Note -- check out the sandbox util (policycoreutils-python) with the X capabilities (policycoreutils-sandbox).  Provides a great tool for running individual processes under extremely tight lock-down.]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Distributed File System Choices: Red Hat Storage, GFS2, &amp; pNFS]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2012/06/27/distributed-file-system-choices-red-hat-storage-gfs2-pnfs/</link>
  <pubDate>Wed, 27 Jun 2012 18:55:40 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://linux.uits.uconn.edu/mas02041/?p=130</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Red Hat has several options for storage needs -- GFS2, CIFS, (p)NFS, Gluster.  It's all about right tool for the job.

<a href="http://www.redhat.com/summit/sessions/index.html#103">http://www.redhat.com/summit/sessions/index.html#103</a>
<h1>RHEL Resilient Storage - GFS2</h1>
Shared storage, scales up to 100TB per instance, supports 2-16 nodes in the cluster, x86_64 only.

Performance is directly related to server and storage class, and to access sets -- best case is where each node generally access its own filesystem area, worst case is where nodes are fighting for locks of the same files/directories.
<h1>RHEL NFS Client</h1>
It's an NFS client -- what else needs to be said?
<h1>pNFS</h1>
Metadata server tells you where to go to get your data, and can even reference data across multiple-tiers -- NFS or directly to the SAN.

Performance can be enhanced with large write caches, internally tiered storage, and is suitable for most workloads, even transactional DBs (using O_DIRECT).  Not so good for highly random read workloads.
<h1>Red Hat Storage (Gluster + utilities, support, and blueprint)</h1>
RHS is a standalone product.  RHS servers in supported config are RAID6+local storage, XFS filesystems, dedicated RHEL6.x and Gluster software, all deployed on commodity hardware (reducing cost).

Clients use Gluster for scalability; NFS or CIFS for simplicity.

Performance improved by scaling horizontally across servers.  But, there is no write cache, and Gluster is a user=space filesystem with some overhead from context switching.  Likely not suitable for big I/O (databases, mail servers), but great for big unstructured data.

Scales to 6PB (or 3PB mirrored), and can add capacity dynamically.
<h1></h1>]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Application High Availability in Virtual Environments]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2012/06/27/application-high-availability-in-virtual-environments/</link>
  <pubDate>Wed, 27 Jun 2012 21:04:51 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://linux.uits.uconn.edu/mas02041/?p=139</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[http://www.redhat.com/summit/sessions/index.html#394

Great discussion around Red Hat's solutions for clustering, fencing, etc, in virtualized environments.

Fencing is /very/ important for shared resources, especially disk.  In a virtualized world (RHEV, VMWare, etc), fencing tools can reach right into the hypervisor to kill a failed node in a cluster.  Similarly, ILO, RSA, DRAC, etc can be used to kill power to physical servers.  Either way, before another node in a cluster takes over the shared resource, it is *critical* that the other node is killed.  But obviously -- this is an easy way to shoot yourself in the foot.  As the presentors just said - "test, test, and test some more" to make sure you fencing parameters align with your deployment.]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[KVM Technology Review and Roadmap Update]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2012/06/28/kvm-technology-review-and-roadmap-update/</link>
  <pubDate>Thu, 28 Jun 2012 17:39:51 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://linux.uits.uconn.edu/mas02041/?p=149</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<ul>
	<li><span style="line-height:15px;">KVM is a relatively small piece of code, leveraging Linux for much functionality.  This makes KVM easy to secure and very flexible in meeting future needs.</span></li>
	<li><span style="line-height:15px;">Leveraging Linux means that KVM automatically gains the power of Linux's hardware support, memory management, network utilities, cgroups, SELinux, etc.</span></li>
	<li><span style="line-height:15px;">Features: RHEL6.3 KVM has all the features of modern hypervisors, without needing 3rd party tools: live snapshots, virtualized disk drivers (VIRTIO), live migration, live <strong><em>block</em></strong> migration, USB passthrough, guest power management, etc.</span></li>
	<li><span style="line-height:15px;">Performance: RHEL 6.3 + KVM holds the <strong>top 7</strong> SPECvirt spots on HP and IBM hardware, with metrics showing ~20%+ better performance than VMWare.</span></li>
	<li><span style="line-height:15px;">Single Guest Scalability: Now supports 160 vCPUs and 2TB RAM per guest (with no additional licensing costs!)</span></li>
	<li><span style="line-height:15px;">RHEL 7.0 will include virtual PCI bridges and will have a new Virtio-SCSI block device, enabling thousands of devices per virtual machine.</span></li>
	<li><span style="line-height:15px;">RHEV scales up to 200 host nodes per cluster.</span></li>
	<li><span style="line-height:15px;"><a href="http://www.vmware.com/pdf/vsphere5/r50/vsphere-50-configuration-maximums.pdf">Compare the above numbers with VMWare</a></span></li>
	<li><strong>KVM has achieved World Record IOPS:</strong> 1,402,720 IOPS on a IBM x3850 X5 for 8KB request using 7 SCSI pass-through devices.. For 1 KB requests, can achieve 1.65M IOPS.</li>
	<li>RHEV 7 will support Windows power virtualization</li>
	<li>RHEL 6.3 brings vCPU and memory hotplug to guests</li>
	<li>KVM has achieved CC-EAL4+ certification with RHEV 5, and is in process of certification with RHEV 6, with sVirt (SELinux wrapped around guests).</li>
	<li>Decommissioned guest storage can be <em>scrubbed</em>, meeting PCI-DSS standards.</li>
	<li>Open Virtualization Alliance promotes open source virtualization and KVM ecosystem.</li>
</ul>]]></content:encoded>

  <title><![CDATA[Migrating Workloads to Red Hat Enterprise Virtualization -  a Customer Perspective]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2012/06/28/migrating-workloads-to-red-hat-enterprise-virtualization-a-customer-perspective/</link>
  <pubDate>Thu, 28 Jun 2012 18:39:50 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://linux.uits.uconn.edu/mas02041/?p=155</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Presentation by Qualcomm on their experience migrating from Xen/RHEL5 to KVM/RHEV6.

Straightforward advice -- plan, plan, plan, then do.

Qualcomm reduced hardware deployment significantly and simplified management with the RHEV tool suite -- significant operational savings.

Qualcomm made extensive use of the virt-v2v tool, but had to modify it (yay Open Source!) to make it cluster (RHCS) aware.  Modifications are shipping with RHEV 6.3.]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Build a PaaS using Open Source Software]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2012/06/29/build-a-paas-using-open-source-software/</link>
  <pubDate>Fri, 29 Jun 2012 14:11:55 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://linux.uits.uconn.edu/mas02041/?p=184</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Discussion about OpenShift.  OpenShift has been fully open-sourced, available on <a href="http://github.com/openshift">GitHub</a> for local deployment, or directly usable as a hosted <a href="https://openshift.redhat.com/app/">solution</a>.

<strong>Rule #1: IaaS != PaaS</strong>

Virtual machines : Application is not necessarily 1:1

<strong>Rule #2: PaaS is not a silver bullet</strong>

<strong></strong>Great for Self-service deployment of applications, varied volatile workloads (development, testing, scale-up/out), with tightly constrained application rules -- which implies standardized deployments from template.

<strong>Rule #3: PaaS is about developers -- AND OPERATIONS!!!!</strong>

Operations becomes about capacity planning, not ticket-drive activities.

<strong>Rule #4: Be ready to learn</strong>

<strong></strong>Developers want languages variety, scaling models, integration models -- and they want it automagically

Operations want multi-tenancy, familiar installation, and sane configurations -- all reproducible.

<strong>What is an application?</strong>

Runtime (OpenShift cartridges)

Code (One Git repository per application)

<strong>Creating an App</strong>

The rhc tools are used to create a namespace (domain), then an application space which includes a name and cartridge type, and push the code.

<strong>What do you get from public OpenShift?</strong>

<strong></strong>A slice of the server, a private Git repository, deployment access.

The PaaS service is comprised of a Broker (director front-end, RESTful) and Nodes.  Each node has multiple "gears" (containers secured with SELinux, constrained with cgroups, and isolated with Kernel namespaces and Bind Mounts).

<strong>Extending OpenShift</strong>

Custom DNS plugins, auth plugs, security policies, and community cartridges.  Quick-start frameworks can be offered to community too.

<strong>LXC and SELinux are the future for isolating and securing OpenShift...</strong>

... but right now, there are a many moving parts being used to provide isolation and security.

<strong>PaaS demans a new security model</strong>

<strong></strong>DAC just won't cut-it, too complicated for PaaS.  MAC (SELinux!) is necessary.

<strong>Step 1 - Unlearn this (and embrace SELinux)!</strong>
<pre>setenforce 0</pre>
<strong>Step 2 - Learn the 'Z' (to see SELinux contexts)</strong>
<pre>ls -lZ
ps -efZ</pre>
<strong>(Review of SELinux contexts and syntax provided)</strong>

<a href="http://fedoraproject.org/wiki/SELinux">http://fedoraproject.org/wiki/SELinux</a>

<strong>Demo - deployment of WordPress to OpenShift, in a VirtualBox LiveCD</strong>

The OpenShift QuickStart is available here: <a href="https://github.com/openshift/wordpress-example">https://github.com/openshift/wordpress-example</a>]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[July 1 2012 Linux problems?  High CPU/Load?  Probably caused by the Leap Second!]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2012/07/01/july-1-2012-tomcat-problems-high-cpuload-probably-caused-by-the-leap-second/</link>
  <pubDate>Sun, 01 Jul 2012 12:43:10 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://linux.uits.uconn.edu/mas02041/?p=194</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<em><strong>(Update posted, see below)</strong></em>

As posted in multiple places around the web:
<h1>Debian</h1>
<pre>/etc/init.d/ntp stop
date `date +"%m%d%H%M%C%y.%S"`</pre>
<h1>Red Hat</h1>
<pre>/etc/init.d/ntpd stop
date `date +"%m%d%H%M%C%y.%S"`</pre>
<strong>Update:</strong>

This first manifested itself for us in our Java stacks -- all of our (dual processor) Tomcat servers were running at a load of 30-40.  However, this is a known (and fixed) kernel bug:

<a href="https://git.kernel.org/?p=linux/kernel/git/torvalds/linux-2.6.git;a=commit;h=6b43ae8a619d17c4935c3320d2ef9e92bdeed05d">https://git.kernel.org/?p=linux/kernel/git/torvalds/linux-2.6.git;a=commit;h=6b43ae8a619d17c4935c3320d2ef9e92bdeed05d</a>

Apparently, simply forcing a reset of the date is enough to fix the problem:
<pre>date -s "`date`"</pre>]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Need a quick proxy via SSH?]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2012/09/14/need-a-quick-proxy-via-ssh/</link>
  <pubDate>Sat, 15 Sep 2012 00:40:30 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://linux.uits.uconn.edu/mas02041/?p=220</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Need access to a web site, tucked behind a firewall?  Do you already have a public SSH server set up to allow access, but not sure how to tunnel your HTTP session?  It's easy!
<pre>ssh -N -D 127.0.0.1:8000 &lt;user&gt;@&lt;ssh server fqdn&gt;</pre>
That command sets up a SOCKS4/SOCKS5 proxy.  Just configure your Browser/OS settings to use 127.0.0.1:8000 as a SOCKS proxy, and you can now access any site that your SSH server can reach.]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[OpenLDAP: Bootstrapping a minimal cn=config]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2012/10/01/openldap-bootstrapping-a-minimal-cnconfig/</link>
  <pubDate>Tue, 02 Oct 2012 02:11:31 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://linux.uits.uconn.edu/mas02041/?p=226</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Here is how to bootstrap OpenLDAP's slapd with an absolutely minimal configuration, without needing an intermediate slapd.conf, with one feature -- the local root user (uid=0/gid=0) has "manage" access.  From this point, ldapmodify can be used via ldapi to continue making configuration changes.  This is a good way to start a new server configuration.
<h1>Bootstrap:</h1>
<pre>$&gt; echo 'dn: cn=config
objectClass: olcGlobal
cn: config

dn: olcDatabase={0}config,cn=config
objectClass: olcDatabaseConfig
olcDatabase: {0}config
olcAccess: to dn.subtree="cn=config" by dn=gidNumber=0+uidNumber=0,cn=peercred,cn=external,cn=auth manage by * none
' | slapadd -n0 -F slapd.d</pre>
<h1>Start slapd:</h1>
<pre>#&gt; slapd -F slapd.d -h ldapi://foo</pre>
<h1>Make changes via ldapmodify:</h1>
<pre>#&gt; ldapmodify -Y EXTERNAL -H ldapi://foo ...</pre>]]></content:encoded>

  <title><![CDATA[So long, and Thanks for All the Fish]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2012/12/20/so-long-and-thanks-for-all-the-fish/</link>
  <pubDate>Thu, 20 Dec 2012 17:00:06 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=237</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[As I sent out to several mailing lists on 2012-12-20:
<blockquote><em>Friends,
</em>
<em>January 24th will be my last day at the University of Connecticut.  I have been offered, and have accepted, a fantastic opportunity with Red Hat Inc.  Having been in IT at UConn since 1996, this was not an easy decision for me - but this is an opportunity that I just cannot pass up.  I have truly enjoyed working with and learning from you all; I will miss you all greatly.
</em>
<em>The Linux/Virtualization team here is establishing a transition plan to maintain service levels.  I will also be working with UITS Leadership to reduce the impact of my leaving on various organizational activities and initiatives.  If you have any concerns or questions, please let me know -- I'd like to leave as few "loose-ends" as possible when I leave.</em>
<em>
I will remain local, so please do keep in touch.  My personal email address will continue to be <a href="mailto:matt@forsetti.com">matt@forsetti.com</a> .</em></blockquote>
So farewell UConn! <a href="http://redhat.com">Red Hat</a>, here I come!

<em>And for those that can't use Google: </em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/So_Long,_and_Thanks_for_All_the_Fish">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/So_Long,_and_Thanks_for_All_the_Fish</a>]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[It's So Hard to Say Goodbye to Yesterday]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2013/01/17/its-so-hard-to-say-goodbye-to-yesterday/</link>
  <pubDate>Fri, 18 Jan 2013 03:00:11 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=262</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<div style="clear:both;">

<img class="alignleft" alt="Cake" src="http://forsetti.files.wordpress.com/2013/01/2013-01-17-14-04-10.jpg?w=225" width="200" height="267" />

&nbsp;
&nbsp;
&nbsp;
&nbsp;

<strong>Check out this incredible cake!!  This was made by Roseanne, wife of my friend Mark.  </strong><em>@RedHat -- do you need someone to bake for your events?</em>

</div>
<div style="clear:both;">

<img class="alignright" alt="Motley Crue" src="http://forsetti.files.wordpress.com/2013/01/2013-01-17-14-03-40.jpg?w=300" width="225" height="169" />

As great as it is to be heading off to Red Hat to start a new chapter of my life, it truly is difficult to leave my many friends at UConn. Again, <em><strong>thank you all</strong></em> -- for the party, and for 17 years of sharing, mentoring, and friendship as we worked for the greater good of the University.  You will all be missed - please keep in touch!
<p style="clear:both;text-align:center;vertical-align:bottom;"><img class="alignleft" alt="Cake" src="http://forsetti.files.wordpress.com/2013/01/2013-01-17-14-03-19.jpg?w=300" width="225" height="169" /></p>

</div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div style="clear:both;">

<strong>[UPDATE 2013-01-18 13:54] The following was just posted to the weekly IT Employee Newsletter:</strong>
<blockquote>Matt Smith, a dedicated professional and highly respected employee of UITS announced his resignation from the University effective January 24th. Matt joined UITS as a student employee supporting desktops and distributed servers in 1996. In those 17 years he developed vast knowledge and technical ability. Under his leadership and “can-do” attitude many technical solutions were researched and implemented. Matt’s expertise made him highly sought after over the years by top leaders in the public and private sectors. Matt has always had a strong commitment to UConn; however, opportunity knocked and he decided to leave and accept a job offer with Red Hat, Inc. Matt will be missed by the University community, his friends and colleagues at UITS, and especially by the Linux team he led since 2009. Please join us in wishing Matt and his family all the best.</blockquote>
</div>]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Done with New Hire Orientation]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2013/02/13/done-with-new-hire-orientation/</link>
  <pubDate>Wed, 13 Feb 2013 22:33:36 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=308</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Time to update the WordPress headshot -- I now have my Red Fedora!

<a href="http://forsetti.files.wordpress.com/2013/02/rhface1.png"><img class=" wp-image-314 alignleft" style="margin-right:30px;" alt="rhface" src="http://forsetti.files.wordpress.com/2013/02/rhface1.png?w=296" width="160" /></a>I'm sitting in the RDU airport, wrapping up three days in Raleigh - two for New Hire Orientation, and one for training on the sales tool.  Ran into David Huff today in the new Red Hat Tower, who echoed a common theme that I'm hearing from Red Hat'rs, triggering this blog post.  Here's a few of the things I've heard:
<ul>
	<li>"I love it here!"</li>
	<li>"This is an awesome place!"</li>
	<li>"I'm where I want to be!"</li>
</ul>
There is an excitement here, people working towards common goals, within a common set of philosophies, understanding how they each contribute to the successes of the company.  The work is interesting, challenging, even enjoyable, and the goals are admirable.

Every session at orientation ended with the presenter saying "Welcome to Red Hat!" -- and I believe they mean it!

I am incredibly excited to be on board - what a great place to be!

<em id="__mceDel"><strong>UPDATED WITH SLIGHTLY IMPROVED PHOTO OF MY NEW FEDORA -- THANKS JOSH ;-p</strong>  </em>]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[New York City]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2013/02/28/new-york-city/</link>
  <pubDate>Fri, 01 Mar 2013 01:00:46 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">https://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=330</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<img class="size-full alignright" alt="image" src="http://forsetti.files.wordpress.com/2013/03/wpid-20130227_153336.jpg" width="375" height="500" />

I don't get into New York City too often, but I had the opportunity this week to check out Red Hat's office.  I took the Amtrak in and out of Penn Station, and a quick subway hop to the office.  Just a stone's throw from Wall St, Red Hat is waaaay up on the 24th floor.  I got to meet most of the SA team for the NE region, and we headed over to Suspenders for dinner, follow by drinks at the Pegu Club, and just had a real good time talking for several hours.

What a smart, active bunch -- it is awesome ( but still a bit intimidating ) to be part of this!]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[More time in Raleigh, more snow in Connecticut!]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2013/03/08/more-time-in-raleigh-more-snow-in-connecticut/</link>
  <pubDate>Fri, 08 Mar 2013 05:01:52 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=332</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[So yet again, this blog post comes as I sit in the RDU airport, leaving behind sunny blue skies and ~50 degree temperatures to arrive just before midnight in cold, wet snow. -- it is good to get home!  I've been in Raleigh again for a couple days, this time for Red Hat IdM (Identity Management( training, and Value Selling training.  Both were great sessions -- of course I enjoy good technical sessions, but I learned quite a bit from the Sales class too.  It really opened my eyes to a few bad habits, which I can now work to address.
<a href="http://forsetti.files.wordpress.com/2013/03/wpid-20130307_1809281.jpg"><img title="20130307_180928.jpg" class="alignright size-full" alt="image" src="http://forsetti.files.wordpress.com/2013/03/wpid-20130307_1809281.jpg" /></a> 


<a href="https://access.redhat.com/knowledge/docs/en-US/Red_Hat_Enterprise_Linux/6/html-single/Identity_Management_Guide/index.html">Red Hat's IdM</a> is pretty cool -- I wish I had know more about it sooner.  It comes with Red Hat Enterprise Linux, and provides a unified LDAP, Kerberos, DNS, and CA solution.  Plus, when used in combination with SSSD, you get a nicely managed user/group/policy solution consistent across your RHEL servers and desktops (and to a lesser extent, other *nix systems that support Kerberos and LDAP).  I have been a longtime proponent of OpenLDAP and MIT Kerberos, and still believe they are each very powerful solutions -- but Red Hat's IdM is a very cohesive suite that ties multiple functions and technologies together <strong>very</strong> well.

Now to really get down to studying for the upcoming RHCE exam -- wish me luck!]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[About Me]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/about-me/</link>
  <pubDate>Tue, 26 May 2015 19:16:05 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">https://forsetti.wordpress.com/?page_id=525</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<img class="alignleft wp-image-526 size-medium" src="https://forsetti.files.wordpress.com/2015/05/headshot.png?w=300" alt="headshot" width="300" height="286"><a href="https://forsetti.files.wordpress.com/2015/05/headshot.png"></a>

<b>Matt Smith</b>
<i>Chief Architect</i>
<i>Northeast Commercial, Red Hat, Inc.</i>

As Chief Architect, Matt is responsible for helping Red Hat customers achieve their key business transformation initiatives through open source architectures and technologies. He regularly advises Fortune 100 enterprises across a wide range of industries, on topics such as Digital Transformation, IT Modernization, and the changing landscape of Security and Risk in Technology. Matt joined Red Hat in 2013, bringing two decades of IT experience ranging from development and system administration to management, strategy, and architecture. Matt has knowledge in many areas of technology, with a particular interest in identity &amp; access management and managing the risk of change, and greatly enjoys bringing Open Source solutions to the enterprise.]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[One Year]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2014/01/28/one-year/</link>
  <pubDate>Wed, 29 Jan 2014 03:19:16 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=122</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Today marks the completion of a <a href="http://forsetti.wordpress.com/2013/01/28/redhatjobs-today-was-my-first-day-and-i-cant-wait-to-go-back-tomorrow/">full year at Red Hat</a>.  It has been an incredible year!  I find myself positively challenged daily, working with some of the smartest people I have ever met -- both my colleagues within Red Hat, and the customers I get to work with.  I get the privilege of being able to work with Open Source technologies, and bring them to a great variety of enterprises.  And I have even found my niche within Red Hat's Identity Management space (imagine that!).

I do miss my team and other <a href="http://forsetti.wordpress.com/2013/01/17/its-so-hard-to-say-goodbye-to-yesterday/">friends at UConn</a>.  UConn gave me great opportunity, both to gain strong technical skills, and to experience leadership, both formally as management, and informally as a mentor and vocal innovator.  I place great value in those first 13 years of my professional career, and I look forward to achieving similar gains in my next 13 years!]]></content:encoded>

  <title><![CDATA[An ode to 130-064-785]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2013/04/19/349/</link>

  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<em>9 digits, what do they mean to me? They are a milestone, for now I am an <a href="https://www.redhat.com/wapps/training/certification/verify.html?certNumber=130-064-785"> RHCE</a>!</em>

OK, so I'm no poet - but thankfully that is not a requirement at Red Hat!  This week I took RH300, and today I passed both EX200 (RHCSA) and EX300 (RHCE). And so now, waiting for my flight out of DC, I celebrate:

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Using Google Chromecast from Fedora 19]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2013/08/25/using-google-chromecast-from-fedora-19/</link>
  <pubDate>Sun, 25 Aug 2013 21:27:24 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=352</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Using Chrome's <a href="https://chrome.google.com/webstore/detail/google-cast/boadgeojelhgndaghljhdicfkmllpafd?hl=en">Google Cast</a> with the <a href="http://google.com/chromecast">Google Chromecast</a> from an IPTables-enabled Linux distribution can be a bit tricky.

The extension starts by issuing an SSDP request from a local ephemeral UDP port to 239.255.255.250 port 1900.  The Chromecast will respond from its IP and another ephemeral UDP port, back to your source UDP port.

IPTables cannot track this simply as "RELATED", given that the target of the first packet is the multicast address, while the source of the response packet is the Chromecast's IP.  And unfortunately, there is no SSDP conntrack module (at least, not that I am aware of, at the time of writing this post).

Therefore, the best we can do for now is to open the ephemeral port range on the client machine.  The list of ephemeral ports, as defined by your Linux machine, can be found by:

<pre>cat /proc/sys/net/ipv4/ip_local_port_range</pre>

Fedora19 uses firewalld, so you will want to use the following:

<pre>firewall-cmd --permanent --add-port=32768-61000/udp
firewall-cmd --reload</pre>

Given the wide range of ports being opened, you may want to restrict access to just your local network. Consider using Network Manager to associate your NIC (eth0,wlan0, whatever) with your "home" zone, and use the following command instead of the above:

<pre>firewall-cmd --permanent --zone=home --add-port=32768-61000/udp</pre>

On non-firewalld systems, use this IPTables one-liner (modifying 192.168.0.0/24 as appropriate for your home network):

<pre>iptables -A INPUT -s 192.168.0.0/24 -p udp -m udp --dport 32768:61000 -j ACCEPT</pre>

Launch Chrome, click the Cast extensions, and it should now "Just Work".  And if it doesn't .... please let me know in the comments on this post.

<strong>UPDATE 20150803 - A few additional notes inspired by the great comment from <a id="commentauthor-1537" href="http://gravatar.com/mauriciograciag">mauriciograciag</a>:</strong>

Note the use of the "--zone=home" parameter in the second firewall-cmd example above.  This <em>can</em> be a more secure option, but does require ensuring that you have a zone named "home", and that Network Manager associates your active network profile with this zone.  If the Network Manager configuration is in place, I do recommend using that option for those working from systems (laptops) that might also find themselves on alternate networks.  However, for desktops that will not be connected to other networks, use of a zone will likely not add any tangible benefits.

If you set these rules via firewall-cmd and wish to revert them, the following should do the trick:

<pre>firewall-cmd –permanent –remove-port=32768-61000/udp
firewall-cmd –permanent –zone=home –remove-port=32768-61000/udp
firewall-cmd –reload</pre>

And lastly, note that the iptables rule must be run as root. Typically, this will be done by updating the iptables startup configuration for your favorite Linux distribution (e.g., you may need to add that line to /etc/iptables); but you might also want to run this a single time, non-persistently, from the command line.  If you have configured sudo appropriately on your Linux systems, preceding the iptables command with "sudo" would work as follows:

<pre>sudo iptables -A INPUT -s 192.168.0.0/24 -p udp -m udp --dport 32768:61000 -j ACCEPT</pre>]]></content:encoded>

    <wp:comment_content><![CDATA[Check out the "interfaces" in the "home" zone.  You'll want to make sure your NIC (e.g., eth0, wlan0) belongs to "home".

Alternatively, public is fine too - all depends on how you want your firewall security configured.  " public" means all the networks connected to your computer have access, "home" means only the networks connected to the listed interfaces have access.  For most people at home, these two models are the same.]]></wp:comment_content>

    <wp:comment_author><![CDATA[Matt]]></wp:comment_author>

    <wp:comment_date>2014-03-30 15:08:27</wp:comment_date>
    <wp:comment_date_gmt>2014-03-30 19:08:27</wp:comment_date_gmt>
    <wp:comment_content><![CDATA[A couple things:
"Runtime" represents how things are running, right this very moment.
"Permanent" represents what is stored in the config files.  This might not yet have taken effect (that is, become "runtime"), but it will take affect the next time you "reload" the firewarell, or reboot the box.

So, to make sure your interface stays in the "home" zone, you'll want to configure that under the "permanent" settings.  But, even better -- go into "Settings"-&gt;"Network", click the gear icon for your NIC, select the "Identity" option on the left, and change the firewall zone for your NIC to home.  That will set this as your permanent zone for the NIC, and will take affect next time you reload, reconnect, or reboot -- I'd recommend just rebooting for simplicity.]]></wp:comment_content>

    <wp:comment_content><![CDATA[IMHO

you need to to include a SUDO to the IPTABLES command

     sudo iptables -A INPUT -s 192.168.0.0/24 -p udp -m udp --dport 32768:61000 -j ACCEPT

And other improvements for this post are

Mention the most secure option first
    firewall-cmd --permanent --zone=home --add-port=32768-61000/udp

and then mention that if after trying this it still does not work then you should try the other commands 

   firewall-cmd --permanent --add-port=32768-61000/udp

If someone added all this new rules and did not work or just wants to reverse them to avoid security issues execute this commands

    firewall-cmd --permanent --remove-port=32768-61000/udp
    firewall-cmd --permanent --zone=home --remove-port=32768-61000/udp
    firewall-cmd --reload]]></wp:comment_content>

OK, so maybe it is still a pretty short list of apps that work on the Chromecast .... but it's only $35 !!!  Pandora has now joined the list:

<ul>
<li>Google Play Music and Video</li>
<li>YouTube</li>
<li>Netflix</li>
<li>Hulu+</li>
<li>Pandora</li>
</ul>

I'm getting pretty darn close to canceling ( or at least reducing ) the cable package!!!]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Recover a Corrupted Partition Table]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2013/11/18/recover-a-corrupted-partition-table/</link>
  <pubDate>Tue, 19 Nov 2013 03:09:25 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=389</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[You ever have one of those days, where you are happily using your Linux laptop, shut it down for transportation, then fire it up and it just doesn't boot?  I'll admit that I do all sorts of screwy things on my laptop, so this is probably my own fault, but I figured I'd write it up in the hopes that it helps someone else, and to give a couple shout-outs to helpful resources.

I am running a Lenovo Thinkpad T430s with Red Hat Enterprise Linux 6.4 Server.  I have a 256GB SSD, which has a 512M boot partition, and the rest is a LUKS encrypted PV, with several LVs for my filesystems.

I booted the laptop and received a black screen with only two words in white on it:

<pre><span style="line-height:1.5;">
Error 17

</span></pre>

Yep, that's it, nothing more. Guessing that "Error 17" is a GRUB error (confirmed by Google),  I grab my trusty RHEL6.4 USB installer (created with livecd-tools), and boot into rescue mode.  A quick fdisk -l shows me a perfectly valid partition layout with a single ~5.5GB partition .... wait, that doesn't seem right.

So, let me go check my partition table backup that I make periodically.  Heh, yeah right.

I vaguely recall my drive layout was as I described above in the second paragraph, but how the heck am I suppose to confirm if my data is still in place (please let this be just a corrupt partition table!!!), and then figure out the partition boundaries and reconstruct the table?

Googling brought me to a <a href="http://www.dedoimedo.com/computers/linux-data-recovery.html">posting on Dedoimedo</a>, which pointed me to the awesome <a href="http://www.cgsecurity.org/wiki/TestDisk_Step_By_Step">TestDisk</a> utility by <a href="http://www.cgsecurity.org/">CGSecurity</a>.  I grabbed the Linux x86_64 binary package provided by the site, and followed the CGSecurity TestDisk instructions to analyze my disk.  Sure enough, it found the first partition 512MB, and then a second 2MB partition.  I read a little about how TestDisk works, looking for signatures for ext2,ext3,ext4,FAT, btrfs, etc., so I understood that it found my first partition which was ext4, but it didn't know what to do with my LUKS partition.  I was pretty sure that the first one was correct, though, so I went ahead and let TestDisk write a new partition table.

Then I fired up fdisk -cu , and deleted the 2nd partition, then recreated it starting at the sector following the first partition, and extending to the last sector on the disk.  I was pretty certain that I had used the rest of the disk .... but still added a healthy dose of finger-crossing.

I wrote the disk partition out, rebooted into rescue mode again, found my way to a shell, and attempted to mount the first partition - success!

Next, I proceeded stepwise to access the second partition:

<pre><span style="font-family:Consolas, Monaco, monospace;font-size:12px;line-height:18px;">#&gt; cryptsetup isLuks /dev/sda2           #First, test to make sure this is recognized as a LUKS device -- it was!</span>
<span style="font-family:Consolas, Monaco, monospace;font-size:12px;line-height:18px;">#&gt; cryptsetup luksOpen /dev/sda2 foo     #Open the LUKS encrypted device as new device "foo"</span>
#&gt; pvs                                   #Scan for LVM PVs, which foo should be -- and it was found!
#&gt; vgchange -ay                          #Activate all LVM VGs on discovered PVs -- and my VG was found!
#&gt; lvs                                   #Scan for LVM LVs -- and mine were found</pre>

At this point, I mounted my home LV, and was able to take a backup. Feeling brave, I gave the laptop a reboot, and everything is running as before.

<strong>So, thank you to Dedoimedo for pointing me towards <a href="http://www.cgsecurity.org/wiki/TestDisk_Step_By_Step">TestDisk</a>, and thank you to CGSecurity for the great tool!</strong>]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[qif-util]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2013/12/31/qif-util/</link>
  <pubDate>Tue, 31 Dec 2013 23:14:50 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">https://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=396</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[I've just posted the first (and likely only) <a href="https://github.com/forsetti/qif-util">release of qif-util</a> .

qif-utils is a very small set of small tools to manipulate QIF files, written sloppily in a very small amount of time.

Having used <a href="http://gnucash.org">Gnucash</a> very happily for a decade, I've always been irritated by the manual method I needed to perform to map transactions to accounts.  <a href="https://github.com/forsetti/qif-util">qif-util</a> was written to automate some of that process, and has been posted in case anyone else can benefit from it.  However, do not rely on this code for anything significant -- only about 20 hours was put into development, most being spent tweaking the output process to give me something usable.

<a href="https://github.com/forsetti/qif-util">qif-util</a> currently provides 3 command line tools, written in Python - see the <a href="https://github.com/forsetti/qif-util/blob/master/README">README</a> for more information.

<ul>
<li>qif-augment inserts account name and type, and an initial transaction representing the opening balance of an account</li>
<li>qif-csv converts a QIF file into CSV (with columns hardcoded into the code)</li>
<li>qif-rules accepts a file of rules describing how to assign a category ("L" QIF code) to QIF transactions, based on matching fields in the transaction.  See the <a href="https://github.com/forsetti/qif-util/blob/master/doc/test.rules">sample rules file</a> for more information.</li>
</ul>

Why post such a trivial and unsupported set of tools?  Well, we are entering tax season in the U.S., QIF is still the most common financial export format out there, and I work for the <a href="http://redhat.com">best open source company</a> out there and fully believe in sharing with the community.  So, if you find value out of these, please drop me a comment here.]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Need to deploy a quick JASIG CAS demo? Try jasig-cas-quickdemo!]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2014/01/05/need-to-deploy-a-quick-jasig-cas-demo-try-jasig-cas-quickdemo/</link>
  <pubDate>Sun, 05 Jan 2014 22:36:52 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=399</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[My development activities on <a href="https://github.com/jasig/mod_auth_cas">mod_auth_cas</a> have significantly reduced over the past couple of years.  In trying to reinvigorate my development cycles around mod_auth_cas, I needed to deploy a CAS server.  Deploying CAS is already <a href="https://wiki.jasig.org/display/CASUM/Demo">pretty straightforward</a> -- install Tomcat, generate SSL certs, deploy the CAS war, and enjoy.  However, I wanted to automate this process to make it easier to repeatedly redeploy a completely clean environment.

So, I have just posted <a href="https://github.com/forsetti/jasig-cas-quickdemo">jasig-cas-quickdemo</a> to GitHub.

jasig-cas-quickdemo is a Maven project that does the following:
<ul>
	<li>Downloads and builds the JASIG CAS project,</li>
	<li>Uses the <a href="http://tomcat.apache.org/maven-plugin.html">Maven Tomcat plugin</a>, download deploy Tomcat 7,</li>
	<li>Generates an SSL cert for HTTPS access, and exports the public cert in PEM format for use by CAS clients,</li>
	<li>Launch CAS on the local Tomcat 7 instance, with the default username=passwored authenticationHandler,</li>
	<li>Grants access to the 'admin' account for the Services Management interface.</li>
</ul>
Try the following on Fedora 19+:
<pre>$ yum install maven git
$ git clone https://github.com/forsetti/jasig-cas-quickdemo.git
$ cd jasig-cas-quickdemo
$ mvn integration-test</pre>
Then just point your browser to https://localhost:8443/cas , and you should see the default CAS login screen.

Check out the <a href="https://github.com/forsetti/jasig-cas-quickdemo/blob/master/README.md">README</a> for more information.]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Timestamping your Bash History]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2014/01/06/timestamping-your-bash-history/</link>
  <pubDate>Mon, 06 Jan 2014 19:54:38 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=421</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Great post titled <a href="http://linux.uits.uconn.edu/gms02004/2014/01/06/remember-when-you-issued-that-command/">Remember when you issued that command...?</a> by Mitch @ UConn on how to include the date/time of each command in your Bash history.

My preference is for ISO8601 time formatting, so I am using:

<pre>$&gt; echo 'export HISTTIMEFORMAT="%F %T "' &gt;&gt; ~/.bash_profile</pre>

But of course, any 'date' formatting will work ( see "man date" for more information ).]]></content:encoded>

Luck (and a little bit of Google-fu) just brought me to the <a href="https://extensions.gnome.org/extension/723/pixel-saver/">Pixel Saver</a> Gnome Shell extension. <item>
  <title><![CDATA[One of those "D'oh" moments: chown .*]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2014/02/10/one-of-those-doh-moments-chown/</link>
  <pubDate>Tue, 11 Feb 2014 02:11:32 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=436</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<strong>UPDATED: Repair process below</strong>

Want to change the ownership of all files in a directory to a user named 'foo'?  Think this is a good way to do it?

<pre>chown -R foo * .*</pre>

Yeah, <span style="text-decoration:underline;"><strong>don't do that</strong></span>. In one of those awesomely awful "D'oh" moments, I did that.  Remember: ".*" matches "..".  So that will traverse up one level, and then recursively back down.

And of course .... I ran this in /mnt .   It took me about 5 seconds to realize what I had just done, before hitting ^c.  Yeah, my system is b0rked. :(

<strong>Resolution provided by Simon Sekidde at Red Hat:</strong>

On Red Hat Enterprise Linux (and derivatives), there is an easy way to re-initialize the ownership and permissions of RPM-installed files:

<pre>for i in `rpm -qa`; do  rpm --setugids $i;  rpm --setperms $i; done</pre>

Make sure to use --setugids before --setperms; if the order is reversed, changing the ownership with --setugids will reset any suid/sgid bits set by --setperms.]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Email Productivity Tip: Differentiate email sent "to" you, "cc"'ing you, or including you on a list]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2014/02/17/email-productivity-tip-differentiate-email-sent-to-you-ccing-you-or-including-you-on-a-list/</link>
  <pubDate>Tue, 18 Feb 2014 01:07:24 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=442</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Do you receive hundreds of emails per day, with varying levels of relevance and criticality?  Do you already apply mail filters to route certain lists or senders into folders, but still suffer with trying to triage email that doesn't come from a one of these predefined origins?

There is no magic bullet, but I noticed a simple pattern that has provided me more gains than the low effort it took to implement:</p>

<blockquote>Mail addressed directly to me, where my email address is explicitly in the "To" line, is generally more important to me than email in which I am only in the "CC" line.  Similarly, email received as part of a distribution list, where I do not explicitly appear in either "To" or "CC", is generally of lower importance.</p></blockquote>

&lt;

p&gt;So, I have implemented a very simple set of filters to capitalize on this pattern.  Note that I have done this in the past with Thunderbird, Evolution, Outlook, and most recently with Zimbra.

<ul><li>Email where I am in the "To" field and the sender is one of a very specific set of VIPs (such as my wife or boss) gets highlighted a very bright color, like Yellow or Light Green</li><li>Email where I am in the "To" field gets highlighted a bright color, like Red or Blue</li><li>Email where I am in the "CC" field gets highlighted a lesser color, generally a pastel</li><li>Email where I am in neither the "To" nor the "CC" gets reduced color, such as light gray.</li></ul>

&lt;

p&gt;At a glance, this helps me determine what I should look at immediately, versus what can wait till later and doesn't need to distract me now. YMMV, but I figured I would share.]]></content:encoded>

  <title><![CDATA[Smith Family Distribution 4.0 Released: "Ryan Timothy"]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2014/09/23/smith-family-distribution-4-0-released-ryan-timothy/</link>
  <pubDate>Wed, 24 Sep 2014 01:43:15 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">http://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=466</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[I am pleased to announce the release of the 4.0 edition of our family distribution, code named "Ryan Timothy". 9 months of effort has gone into this release, with the final product weighing in at 7lb 6oz and measuring 20", and with no known bugs at release time! Development Lead Caron made several significant commits through the night, with QA support from Release Engineer Matt.  The team is holding on any further commits for a while, and is doing quite well.  Ryan joins previous releases (codenamed "Marie Caron", "Brianna Theresa", and "Amber Grace") in a full Long Term Support life cycle, with Extended Lifecycle Support as needed.
<img title="wp-1411522767194" class="alignnone size-full" alt="image" width="320" src="http://mjs.fyi/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/wpid-wp-1411522767194.jpeg" /><img title="wp-1411522788344" class="alignnone size-full" alt="image" width="320" src="http://mjs.fyi/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/wpid-wp-1411522788344.jpeg" />]]></content:encoded>

  <title><![CDATA[Configuring firewalld on RHEL7 for Red Hat IdM (FreeIPA)]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2014/12/02/configuring-firewalld-on-rhel7-for-red-hat-idm-freeipa/</link>

  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>

  <content:encoded><![CDATA[The following commands will create the appropriate firewall rules for a RHEL7 system running IdM/FreeIPA

<pre>#&gt; firewall-cmd --permanent --add-service=http --add-service=https --add-service=ldap --add-service=ldaps --add-service=kerberos --add-service=kpasswd --add-service=dns --add-service=ntp

#&gt; firewall-cmd --reload</pre>]]></content:encoded>

  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2015/01/02/el7-on-old-acer-aspire-5520-laptop/</link>
  <pubDate>Fri, 02 Jan 2015 19:37:12 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">https://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=498</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Reminder to self: When installing EL7 (RHEL7/CentOS7) on a old Acer Aspire 5520 laptop, fetch and install the following RPMs from elrepo.org:

<ul>
    <li>kmod-ath5k</li>
    <li>kmod-nvidia-304xx-304</li>
    <li>nvidia-x11-drv-304xx-304</li>
    <li>kmod-forcedeth</li>
</ul>

Output of lspci (to add searchable keywords to this post):

<pre>00:00.0 RAM memory: NVIDIA Corporation MCP67 Memory Controller (rev a2)
00:01.0 ISA bridge: NVIDIA Corporation MCP67 ISA Bridge (rev a2)
00:01.1 SMBus: NVIDIA Corporation MCP67 SMBus (rev a2)
00:01.2 RAM memory: NVIDIA Corporation MCP67 Memory Controller (rev a2)
00:01.3 Co-processor: NVIDIA Corporation MCP67 Co-processor (rev a2)
00:02.0 USB controller: NVIDIA Corporation MCP67 OHCI USB 1.1 Controller (rev a2)
00:02.1 USB controller: NVIDIA Corporation MCP67 EHCI USB 2.0 Controller (rev a2)
00:04.0 USB controller: NVIDIA Corporation MCP67 OHCI USB 1.1 Controller (rev a2)
00:04.1 USB controller: NVIDIA Corporation MCP67 EHCI USB 2.0 Controller (rev a2)
00:06.0 IDE interface: NVIDIA Corporation MCP67 IDE Controller (rev a1)
00:07.0 Audio device: NVIDIA Corporation MCP67 High Definition Audio (rev a1)
00:08.0 PCI bridge: NVIDIA Corporation MCP67 PCI Bridge (rev a2)
00:09.0 IDE interface: NVIDIA Corporation MCP67 AHCI Controller (rev a2)
00:0a.0 Ethernet controller: NVIDIA Corporation MCP67 Ethernet (rev a2)
00:0c.0 PCI bridge: NVIDIA Corporation MCP67 PCI Express Bridge (rev a2)
00:0d.0 PCI bridge: NVIDIA Corporation MCP67 PCI Express Bridge (rev a2)
00:12.0 VGA compatible controller: NVIDIA Corporation C67 [GeForce 7000M / nForce 610M] (rev a2)
00:18.0 Host bridge: Advanced Micro Devices, Inc. [AMD] K8 [Athlon64/Opteron] HyperTransport Technology Configuration
00:18.1 Host bridge: Advanced Micro Devices, Inc. [AMD] K8 [Athlon64/Opteron] Address Map
00:18.2 Host bridge: Advanced Micro Devices, Inc. [AMD] K8 [Athlon64/Opteron] DRAM Controller
00:18.3 Host bridge: Advanced Micro Devices, Inc. [AMD] K8 [Athlon64/Opteron] Miscellaneous Control
01:04.0 FireWire (IEEE 1394): Ricoh Co Ltd R5C832 IEEE 1394 Controller (rev 05)
01:04.1 SD Host controller: Ricoh Co Ltd R5C822 SD/SDIO/MMC/MS/MSPro Host Adapter (rev 22)
01:04.2 System peripheral: Ricoh Co Ltd R5C592 Memory Stick Bus Host Adapter (rev 12)
01:04.3 System peripheral: Ricoh Co Ltd xD-Picture Card Controller (rev 12)
05:00.0 Ethernet controller: Qualcomm Atheros AR242x / AR542x Wireless Network Adapter (PCI-Express) (rev 01)</pre>

Closing thought: EL7 runs <em>really</em> well on this ancient laptop!]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Chrome as your desktop environment]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2015/01/03/chrome-as-your-desktop-environment/</link>
  <pubDate>Sat, 03 Jan 2015 17:06:19 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">https://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=500</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[I have an old Asus 1000HE Eee PC (32-bit, 2GB RAM, 128GB SSD).  Fedora 21 with Gnome Shell runs <em>ok</em>, but as I only use this netbook to browse the web, I figured I'd try to figure out how to launch Chrome as the desktop environment, cutting Gnome Shell out completely.  In the process of trying to determine which lightweight window manager I would use, I discovered that Google Chrome (my browser of choice) includes it's own called <em>Ash</em>.  So, setting this up was as easy as creating a single file, and simply selecting "Chrome" as my login session from GDM.

<strong>/etc/X11/sessions/chrome.desktop</strong>

<pre>[Desktop Entry]
Name=Chrome
Comment=Chrome Browser
Exec=/usr/bin/google-chrome --open-ash --ash-force-desktop --ash-host-window-bounds="1024x600"
TryExec=/usr/bin/google-chrome
Icon=
Type=Application
DesktopNames=Chrome</pre>

This isn't a <em>perfect</em> solution, but it's pretty neat.  A few problems:

<ul>
    <li>Triggering a download crashes the browser, as Chrome can't launch the Gnome (GTK?) download widget</li>
    <li>No battery,audio, or wifi system icons</li>
    <li>Initially, the desktop was off-center.  Apparently, the netbook screen size (1024x600) was odd, so it needed to be explicitly specified with the "-bounds" parameter.</li>
</ul>

Next, I'm going to give <a href="http://www.android-x86.org/">Android-x86</a> a shot on this Eee PC.]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[An Introduction to Interoperability Challenges in the Modern Enterprise]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2015/01/21/an-introduction-to-interoperability-challenges-in-the-modern-enterprise/</link>
  <pubDate>Wed, 21 Jan 2015 22:42:14 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">https://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=502</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[<blockquote>The act of hooking-up a dishwasher is not unlike adding a Linux system to an existing enterprise IT environment. When you deploy a Linux system, it too needs to be “hooked-up”. As the data that flows through your environment consists of different kinds of objects (e.g. users, groups, hosts, and services) the associated identity information is not unlike the water in your dishwasher. Without this identity information(or, in the case of the dishwasher, without the water) your new machine is less than useful. Ensuring that your new system (or new dishwasher) is properly connected is important, if not essential.</blockquote>

Read more at the <a href="http://rhelblog.redhat.com/2015/01/20/an-introduction-to-interoperability-challenges-in-the-modern-enterprise/">Red Hat Enterprise Linux Blog</a>.]]></content:encoded>

<item>
  <title><![CDATA[Making a backup of your DD-WRT config]]></title>
  <link>http://mjs.fyi/2015/03/01/making-a-backup-of-your-dd-wrt-config/</link>
  <pubDate>Mon, 02 Mar 2015 02:27:27 +0000</pubDate>
  <dc:creator>forsetti</dc:creator>
  <guid isPermaLink="false">https://forsetti.wordpress.com/?p=507</guid>
  <description/>
  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Assuming you have ssh enabled on your <a href="http://www.dd-wrt.com/">DD-WRT</a>, and that your DD-WRT is at ddwrt.lab.test, the following will generate a shell script that can be used to restore your config:

<pre>ssh root@ddwrt.lab.test 'nvram show | grep = | cut -d"=" -f 1 | while read key; do echo nvram set $key="$(nvram get $key)"; done' &gt; ddwrt.config</pre>

Restore is then simply:

<pre>cat ddwrt.config | ssh -q root@ddwrt.lab.test</pre>]]></content:encoded>

  <content:encoded><![CDATA[Two years at Red Hat have flown by at such a rapid pace; I'm even a couple months late in blogging about it!  To top off another year full of great projects, awesome technology, and incredible people, I've had a couple exciting things happen that I wanted to share.

Last month I was chosen to be a "Red Hat Chairman's Award" recipient, and last week (photo below) I was awarded "North American Account Solutions Architect" of the Year!

<a href="http://forsetti.files.wordpress.com/2015/04/wpid-f_dsc01831.jpg"><img title="f_dsc01831.jpg" class="alignnone size-full" alt="image" src="http://forsetti.files.wordpress.com/2015/04/wpid-f_dsc01831.jpg" /></a>

I am truly humbled by both of these awards, and was speechless when I heard my name called (those who know me well understand how rare that is). I am looking forward to see what this next year will bring!]]></content:encoded>
